# Python Statistics Essential Training

> The field of statistics has become increasingly dependent on data analysis and interpretation using Python. With the rise of big data and data science, the demand for professionals who can effectively analyze and interpret data using Python has skyrocketed. In this course, Matt Harrison teaches you how to collect, clean, analyze, and visualize data using the powerful tools of the Python programmin

> **[2:06](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/python-statistics-essential-training-19258005/loading-data?u=76281980&t=126)** I'm passing in a URL.
>
> **[2:07](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/python-statistics-essential-training-19258005/loading-data?u=76281980&t=127)** We've got this locally on our code space.
>
> **[2:10](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/python-statistics-essential-training-19258005/loading-data?u=76281980&t=130)** I also have a variable pointing to where you can get this on the internet if you want to as well.
>
> **[2:16](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/python-statistics-essential-training-19258005/loading-data?u=76281980&t=136)** When you look at this read_CSV function, this probably looks different if you're used to Pandas One with the two parameters, engine and dtype_backend.
>
> **[2:25](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/python-statistics-essential-training-19258005/loading-data?u=76281980&t=145)** Both of those we are specifying as pyarrow.
>
> **[2:28](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/python-statistics-essential-training-19258005/loading-data?u=76281980&t=148)** What engine equals pyarrow does is it uses the arrow library to load the CSV file.
>
> **[2:34](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/python-statistics-essential-training-19258005/loading-data?u=76281980&t=154)** This can be a lot quicker than using the standard Pandas logic to do that.
>
> **[2:39](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/python-statistics-essential-training-19258005/loading-data?u=76281980&t=159)** And what dtype_backend does is it allows you to store the data types using the arrow library instead of NumPy, and that gives you a lot of memory benefits as well.
>
> **[2:51](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/python-statistics-essential-training-19258005/loading-data?u=76281980&t=171)** Let's inspect the shape of this dataset.
>
> **[2:53](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/python-statistics-essential-training-19258005/loading-data?u=76281980&t=173)** I'm going to go down and execute this next cell.
>
> **[2:56](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/python-statistics-essential-training-19258005/loading-data?u=76281980&t=176)** Again, holding down, control and hitting enter to run that.
>
> **[3:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/python-statistics-essential-training-19258005/loading-data?u=76281980&t=180)** You can see that this returns a tupple.
>
> **[3:01](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/python-statistics-essential-training-19258005/loading-data?u=76281980&t=181)** There's 2,930 rows and 82 columns.
>
> **[3:06](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/python-statistics-essential-training-19258005/loading-data?u=76281980&t=186)** Let's look at the first few rows of data.
>
> **[3:09](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/python-statistics-essential-training-19258005/loading-data?u=76281980&t=189)** I'm going to use this head method to do that and you can see the first five rows.
>

>
> **[3:16](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/python-statistics-essential-training-19258005/loading-data?u=76281980&t=196)** You can scroll over if you want to and see some of it.
>
> **[3:19](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/python-statistics-essential-training-19258005/loading-data?u=76281980&t=199)** Note that we are getting some of the columns truncated here.
>
> **[3:23](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/python-statistics-essential-training-19258005/loading-data?u=76281980&t=203)** You can see that there's an ellipses.
>
> **[3:25](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/python-statistics-essential-training-19258005/loading-data?u=76281980&t=205)** We're only showing you the first 10 columns and the last 10 columns, but we've got 82 columns of, again, housing sales data from Ames, Iowa.
>
> **[3:36](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/python-statistics-essential-training-19258005/loading-data?u=76281980&t=216)** One of the things I like to do is run the describe method and what the describe method does is gives us summary statistics for each of the numeric columns in the data frame.
>
> **[3:47](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/python-statistics-essential-training-19258005/loading-data?u=76281980&t=227)** In the index now you can see on the left hand side where it says count, mean, STD, min, that is the index of this data frame and indexes don't have to be numeric.
>
> **[3:58](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/python-statistics-essential-training-19258005/loading-data?u=76281980&t=238)** Pandas will let them be non-numeric, and in this case, we can see that we have non-numeric, we have string entries for the index representing the statistical results of each of these numeric columns.
>
> **[4:13](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/python-statistics-essential-training-19258005/loading-data?u=76281980&t=253)** So for order, this is the order of cells, and you can see in the count row, 2,930.
>
> **[4:22](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/python-statistics-essential-training-19258005/loading-data?u=76281980&t=262)** Now, one thing to be aware of with Pandas is that count probably doesn't mean what you think it means.
>
> **[4:27](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/python-statistics-essential-training-19258005/loading-data?u=76281980&t=267)** Count in Pandas means the number of non missing values.
>
> **[4:30](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/python-statistics-essential-training-19258005/loading-data?u=76281980&t=270)** So, if we look over a little bit at the lot frontage count, it says that that is 2,440.
>
> **[4:38](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/python-statistics-essential-training-19258005/loading-data?u=76281980&t=278)** So what that means is that there are 2,440 entries there that had values and that there are a few of them that are missing values.
>
> **[4:46](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/python-statistics-essential-training-19258005/loading-data?u=76281980&t=286)** We also have the mean, the standard deviation, the minimum value, and the maximum value, and then the inner quartiles there.
>
> **[4:53](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/python-statistics-essential-training-19258005/loading-data?u=76281980&t=293)** I like to use this for a gut check to understand what the data looks like.
>
> **[4:58](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/python-statistics-essential-training-19258005/loading-data?u=76281980&t=298)** You can see that a lot of these numeric values don't go very high and they're also non-negative.
>
> **[5:04](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/python-statistics-essential-training-19258005/loading-data?u=76281980&t=304)** By default, Pandas is going to use 64 bits of information to store this data.
>
> **[5:09](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/python-statistics-essential-training-19258005/loading-data?u=76281980&t=309)** We can probably save more memory by changing those types and I'll show how to do that in a future lesson.
>
> **[5:17](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/python-statistics-essential-training-19258005/loading-data?u=76281980&t=317)** In this lesson, we loaded the data for the Ames, Iowa dataset and then we did some basic inspection and sanity checks to make sure that we have the data loaded.

> **[3:40](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/python-statistics-essential-training-19258005/solution-clean-ames?u=76281980&t=220)** And then I'm going to run the cell and make sure that it works.

>
> **[3:48](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/python-statistics-essential-training-19258005/solution-clean-ames?u=76281980&t=228)** I'll just make a new cell below this and look at housing.
>
> **[3:53](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/python-statistics-essential-training-19258005/solution-clean-ames?u=76281980&t=233)** And it looks like it worked.
>
> **[3:55](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/python-statistics-essential-training-19258005/solution-clean-ames?u=76281980&t=235)** In this lesson, I've given you a nice hint for how to make your notebooks a lot easier to use.
>
> **[4:02](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/python-statistics-essential-training-19258005/solution-clean-ames?u=76281980&t=242)** And that is make a chain that cleans up your code, stick that into a function, and then pull that up to the top of your notebook so that when you come back to your notebook, you can easily start going and not have to scroll through and figure out what's going on with your code.

>
> **[0:10](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/python-statistics-essential-training-19258005/outliers-and-z-scores?u=76281980&t=10)** I'll show one using our classic Z score and another one by using the inner quartile range, and we'll look at how we can use the pandas functionality to do that and make functions that you can use and leverage in your own code if you need to calculate values that tend to be on the extremes.
>
> **[0:33](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/python-statistics-essential-training-19258005/outliers-and-z-scores?u=76281980&t=33)** So here is a function right here, calc Z and you'll notice that the input to calc Z, the first parameter is a data frame so a function that has the first parameter is a data frame is a candidate to use with the pipe method.

> **[1:15](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/python-statistics-essential-training-19258005/outliers-and-z-scores?u=76281980&t=75)** This is a case where I'm not using chaining, I'm calculating the mean of this column that I'm passing in here.

> **[1:21](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/python-statistics-essential-training-19258005/outliers-and-z-scores?u=76281980&t=81)** So the DF that is coming in is whatever is passed in from the chain.
>
> **[1:27](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/python-statistics-essential-training-19258005/outliers-and-z-scores?u=76281980&t=87)** In this case, this is the original housing, just coming in as the DF here and sale price is coming in as the column value.
>
> **[1:35](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/python-statistics-essential-training-19258005/outliers-and-z-scores?u=76281980&t=95)** So we first calculate the mean of sale price, then we calculate the standard deviation of sale price and then the Z score is calculated by taking that column, subtracting the mean, and dividing by the standard deviation.
>
> **[1:49](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/python-statistics-essential-training-19258005/outliers-and-z-scores?u=76281980&t=109)** We can leverage pandas to make this a vectorized operation and to make it work relatively easily.
>
> **[1:56](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/python-statistics-essential-training-19258005/outliers-and-z-scores?u=76281980&t=116)** The Z-score gives us a quantification how many standard deviations we are away from the mean and generally an outlier is a value that has an absolute magnitude above three for data that has a normal distribution.
>
> **[2:17](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/python-statistics-essential-training-19258005/outliers-and-z-scores?u=76281980&t=137)** One of the nice things about making this as a function is we can also use it in assign.

>

> **[0:53](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/python-statistics-essential-training-19258005/comparing-two-categoricals?u=76281980&t=53)** Pandas is lazy, when you do a groupby, it does not calculate anything.
>
> **[0:58](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/python-statistics-essential-training-19258005/comparing-two-categoricals?u=76281980&t=58)** So let's do an aggregation to make it run a calculation.

> **[1:01](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/python-statistics-essential-training-19258005/comparing-two-categoricals?u=76281980&t=61)** We're going to say size.
>
> **[1:03](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/python-statistics-essential-training-19258005/comparing-two-categoricals?u=76281980&t=63)** Let's look at the output of this.
>
> **[1:05](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/python-statistics-essential-training-19258005/comparing-two-categoricals?u=76281980&t=65)** This gives us a series and this is a series that has what's called a multi-index or a hierarchical index.
>
> **[1:13](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/python-statistics-essential-training-19258005/comparing-two-categoricals?u=76281980&t=73)** In the left-hand side, we see Overall Qual and Basement Condition.
>
> **[1:17](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/python-statistics-essential-training-19258005/comparing-two-categoricals?u=76281980&t=77)** Those are both in the index, so there are actually two indexes here.
>
> **[1:23](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/python-statistics-essential-training-19258005/comparing-two-categoricals?u=76281980&t=83)** And then zero, zero, zero, three on the right, that's the values in the series.
>
> **[1:29](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/python-statistics-essential-training-19258005/comparing-two-categoricals?u=76281980&t=89)** What I'm going to do now is a pretty powerful and sometimes confusing operation, I'm going to call unstack.
>
> **[1:34](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/python-statistics-essential-training-19258005/comparing-two-categoricals?u=76281980&t=94)** What this is going to do is it's going to take that Basement Condition index.
>
> **[1:39](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/python-statistics-essential-training-19258005/comparing-two-categoricals?u=76281980&t=99)** It's going to rotate it, pull it out, and put it in the columns so every value from Basement Condition will be a unique column.
>
> **[1:48](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/python-statistics-essential-training-19258005/comparing-two-categoricals?u=76281980&t=108)** Let's run that and see what happens.
>
> **[1:50](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/python-statistics-essential-training-19258005/comparing-two-categoricals?u=76281980&t=110)** Look at that, and here is our cross-tabulation.
>
> **[1:54](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/python-statistics-essential-training-19258005/comparing-two-categoricals?u=76281980&t=114)** We have all of the Overall Quality values in the index and we have all of the Basement Conditions in the column.
>
> **[2:02](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/python-statistics-essential-training-19258005/comparing-two-categoricals?u=76281980&t=122)** Because this is a common operation, Pandas actually gives this to us for free with the crosstab function.
>
> **[2:10](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/python-statistics-essential-training-19258005/comparing-two-categoricals?u=76281980&t=130)** We can call crosstab and say in the index I want the Overall Quality column and in the columns I want the Basement Condition column.
>
> **[2:19](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/python-statistics-essential-training-19258005/comparing-two-categoricals?u=76281980&t=139)** You can see that this is the same result that we see up above, but it's one line of code versus those four lines of code up there.
>
> **[2:27](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/python-statistics-essential-training-19258005/comparing-two-categoricals?u=76281980&t=147)** I show you this because again, I think it's useful to have these powers to understand grouping, to understand size and unstack.

### 3. Linear Regressions

> **[1:25](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/python-statistics-essential-training-19258005/linear-regression?u=76281980&t=85)** This X is the features or the columns that we're going to try and use to predict sales price.
>
> **[1:32](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/python-statistics-essential-training-19258005/linear-regression?u=76281980&t=92)** So keeping sales price in there would be cheating if we're training the model with the sales price and asking it to predict the sales price.
>
> **[1:39](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/python-statistics-essential-training-19258005/linear-regression?u=76281980&t=99)** Then the other data set that we need is the target that we want.

> **[1:46](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/python-statistics-essential-training-19258005/linear-regression?u=76281980&t=106)** I'm going to use a function from scikit-learn called train_test_split.
>
> **[1:50](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/python-statistics-essential-training-19258005/linear-regression?u=76281980&t=110)** And what that's going to do is, it's going to take our X and our Y data and split it up.
>
> **[1:55](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/python-statistics-essential-training-19258005/linear-regression?u=76281980&t=115)** We're going to split it up into a training set and a testing set.
>
> **[1:59](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/python-statistics-essential-training-19258005/linear-regression?u=76281980&t=119)** This is very important to do when you're using machine learning.
>
> **[2:02](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/python-statistics-essential-training-19258005/linear-regression?u=76281980&t=122)** The reason is you want to understand how your model performs.
>
> **[2:06](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/python-statistics-essential-training-19258005/linear-regression?u=76281980&t=126)** And if you evaluate your model on data that's seen before, it's pretty trivial to make a model that can perform very well on data that's seen before.
>
> **[2:15](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/python-statistics-essential-training-19258005/linear-regression?u=76281980&t=135)** All it has to do is memorize that data.

> **[5:40](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/python-statistics-essential-training-19258005/linear-regression?u=76281980&t=340)** So the score that comes out of this is often called the R2 or R squared score or the coefficient of determination.
>
> **[5:47](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/python-statistics-essential-training-19258005/linear-regression?u=76281980&t=347)** This is a value between zero, typically, and one.

> **[5:51](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/python-statistics-essential-training-19258005/linear-regression?u=76281980&t=351)** The closer this is to one, the better the model is.
>
> **[5:54](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/python-statistics-essential-training-19258005/linear-regression?u=76281980&t=354)** What this represents is the amount of variance in the label or target that is explained by the features or columns in the data.
>
> **[6:05](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/python-statistics-essential-training-19258005/linear-regression?u=76281980&t=365)** So 84% of the variance in the label that we're trying to predict or that sales price is predicted by the data that we're passing into this.
>

>
> **[6:36](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/python-statistics-essential-training-19258005/linear-regression?u=76281980&t=396)** It might be good enough, it might not be.

> **[3:39](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/python-statistics-essential-training-19258005/interpreting-linear-regression-models?u=76281980&t=219)** What do I mean by that?
>
> **[3:40](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/python-statistics-essential-training-19258005/interpreting-linear-regression-models?u=76281980&t=220)** I mean that you can take this intercept value, and you can take the labels that are passed into the model to make a prediction, multiply them by these coefficients, and if you sum those up, you get the prediction from the model.
>
> **[3:53](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/python-statistics-essential-training-19258005/interpreting-linear-regression-models?u=76281980&t=233)** This turns out to be a nice feature that you can interpret the model.
>
> **[3:57](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/python-statistics-essential-training-19258005/interpreting-linear-regression-models?u=76281980&t=237)** You can understand why it's making the predictions it has.
>
> **[4:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/python-statistics-essential-training-19258005/interpreting-linear-regression-models?u=76281980&t=240)** Also, you can look at the magnitudes of these coefficients.
>
> **[4:03](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/python-statistics-essential-training-19258005/interpreting-linear-regression-models?u=76281980&t=243)** The larger the magnitude of them, the more impact they have on the model.
>
> **[4:07](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/python-statistics-essential-training-19258005/interpreting-linear-regression-models?u=76281980&t=247)** The coefficients that have a small magnitude mean that the feature is not having as much impact on the final prediction.
>
> **[4:15](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/python-statistics-essential-training-19258005/interpreting-linear-regression-models?u=76281980&t=255)** In future lesson, we'll look at different models and compare and contrast those with linear regression.

#### Standardizing values
> [LinkedIn Learning](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/python-statistics-essential-training-19258005/standardizing-values?u=76281980)

> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/python-statistics-essential-training-19258005/standardizing-values?u=76281980&t=0)** - [Instructor] In this lesson, we're going to look at standardizing values.
>
> **[0:04](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/python-statistics-essential-training-19258005/standardizing-values?u=76281980&t=4)** We're going to talk first about what this concept of standardization means, and then we'll learn how to do this from scikit-learn.
>
> **[0:11](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/python-statistics-essential-training-19258005/standardizing-values?u=76281980&t=11)** Again, once you understand the conventions of scikit-learn, this is not particularly hard.

>
> **[0:32](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/python-statistics-essential-training-19258005/standardizing-values?u=76281980&t=32)** And oftentimes, people say normalize, and when they say normalize, they mean standardization.
>
> **[0:38](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/python-statistics-essential-training-19258005/standardizing-values?u=76281980&t=38)** What standardization means is that we're going to take our X or our dataframe and we're going to basically collapse the values or shrink them into a given range or maybe expand them into a given range and center them.
>
> **[0:53](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/python-statistics-essential-training-19258005/standardizing-values?u=76281980&t=53)** More specifically, it means we give each column a mean value of zero and the standard deviation of one.
>
> **[1:02](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/python-statistics-essential-training-19258005/standardizing-values?u=76281980&t=62)** Why is that?
>
> **[1:03](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/python-statistics-essential-training-19258005/standardizing-values?u=76281980&t=63)** You could imagine that certain models are impacted by the scale of the data.
>
> **[1:08](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/python-statistics-essential-training-19258005/standardizing-values?u=76281980&t=68)** And if you have a housing model that's predicting price, it might have one column that represents the number of bathrooms in a house, which might be like from one to five bathrooms, might be the maximum in a house.
>
> **[1:23](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/python-statistics-essential-training-19258005/standardizing-values?u=76281980&t=83)** You might have another column that represents the square footage, which might be from like 200 for a mini house up to like 20,000 for a mansion.
>
> **[1:32](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/python-statistics-essential-training-19258005/standardizing-values?u=76281980&t=92)** The scale of the 20,000, that's a couple order of magnitudes bigger than the number of bathrooms.
>
> **[1:38](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/python-statistics-essential-training-19258005/standardizing-values?u=76281980&t=98)** And certain algorithms are impacted by the relationship between the magnitude of the columns.
>
> **[1:45](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/python-statistics-essential-training-19258005/standardizing-values?u=76281980&t=105)** One of the ways to do that is using standardization.
>
> **[1:49](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/python-statistics-essential-training-19258005/standardizing-values?u=76281980&t=109)** Let's look at how to do this using scikit-learn.
>
> **[1:52](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/python-statistics-essential-training-19258005/standardizing-values?u=76281980&t=112)** There is a pre-processing module in scikit-learn.
>
> **[1:56](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/python-statistics-essential-training-19258005/standardizing-values?u=76281980&t=116)** And you can make an instance of the pre-processing module.
>
> **[2:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/python-statistics-essential-training-19258005/standardizing-values?u=76281980&t=120)** A StandardScaler is a transformer in scikit-learn.
>
> **[2:04](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/python-statistics-essential-training-19258005/standardizing-values?u=76281980&t=124)** That means that it has at least two methods.
>
> **[2:06](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/python-statistics-essential-training-19258005/standardizing-values?u=76281980&t=126)** One is fit and the other is transform.
>
> **[2:09](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/python-statistics-essential-training-19258005/standardizing-values?u=76281980&t=129)** In the case of the training data, we're actually going to call fit_transform, which combines fitting and transforming.
>
> **[2:15](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/python-statistics-essential-training-19258005/standardizing-values?u=76281980&t=135)** So fit trains the model, and then transform takes input and transforms the input to a given output.
>
> **[2:23](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/python-statistics-essential-training-19258005/standardizing-values?u=76281980&t=143)** In the case of a StandardScaler, it takes data after it's been trained, the training tells the model, "Hey, this column should have a mean value of this and this column should have a mean value of this," and it knows how to shrink and move those means around.
>
> **[2:39](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/python-statistics-essential-training-19258005/standardizing-values?u=76281980&t=159)** And then the transform actually does that transform for you.
>
> **[2:43](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/python-statistics-essential-training-19258005/standardizing-values?u=76281980&t=163)** So here we're going to call fit_transform on the training data.
>
> **[2:45](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/python-statistics-essential-training-19258005/standardizing-values?u=76281980&t=165)** This gives us a new X_train.
>

>
> **[2:50](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/python-statistics-essential-training-19258005/standardizing-values?u=76281980&t=170)** We don't want to call fit again on the testing data, we just want to call transform on the testing data.
>
> **[2:56](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/python-statistics-essential-training-19258005/standardizing-values?u=76281980&t=176)** So, once we've made a model, we want to evaluate it on data that it hasn't seen before.

>

#### Regression with XGBoost
> [LinkedIn Learning](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/python-statistics-essential-training-19258005/regression-with-xgboost?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/python-statistics-essential-training-19258005/regression-with-xgboost?u=76281980&t=0)** - [Instructor] In this lesson, we're going to use a very popular library called XGBoost to make a regression model as well.
>
> **[0:08](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/python-statistics-essential-training-19258005/regression-with-xgboost?u=76281980&t=8)** We're going to compare and contrast the performance and the interpretability of XGBoost with linear regression.
>
> **[0:17](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/python-statistics-essential-training-19258005/regression-with-xgboost?u=76281980&t=17)** Let me quickly explain what XGBoost does.
>
> **[0:21](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/python-statistics-essential-training-19258005/regression-with-xgboost?u=76281980&t=21)** It makes a decision tree to make predictions and those predictions, in this case, the price of the house are probably going to be off from the actual values.
>
> **[0:32](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/python-statistics-essential-training-19258005/regression-with-xgboost?u=76281980&t=32)** So the boosting comes from making subsequent trees and the subsequent trees take that same input and try and fix the error.
>
> **[0:42](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/python-statistics-essential-training-19258005/regression-with-xgboost?u=76281980&t=42)** The error is called the residual.
>
> **[0:44](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/python-statistics-essential-training-19258005/regression-with-xgboost?u=76281980&t=44)** And you can add as many trees as you want.
>
> **[0:47](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/python-statistics-essential-training-19258005/regression-with-xgboost?u=76281980&t=47)** Each of those trees keeps fixing that error.
>
> **[0:50](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/python-statistics-essential-training-19258005/regression-with-xgboost?u=76281980&t=50)** I like to compare this to golfing.
>
> **[0:53](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/python-statistics-essential-training-19258005/regression-with-xgboost?u=76281980&t=53)** When you golf generally you don't just hit the ball once.
>
> **[0:57](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/python-statistics-essential-training-19258005/regression-with-xgboost?u=76281980&t=57)** You have multiple times to hit the ball and that's what this is doing.
>
> **[1:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/python-statistics-essential-training-19258005/regression-with-xgboost?u=76281980&t=60)** The first tree hits the ball, so to speak.
>
> **[1:03](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/python-statistics-essential-training-19258005/regression-with-xgboost?u=76281980&t=63)** It's not going to go in the hole probably.
>
> **[1:06](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/python-statistics-essential-training-19258005/regression-with-xgboost?u=76281980&t=66)** It'll be away from the hole by some amount, there'll be some residual and then the next tree will basically take a new club and hit the ball from there and try and fix the error, get it closer to the hole, so to speak.
>
> **[1:19](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/python-statistics-essential-training-19258005/regression-with-xgboost?u=76281980&t=79)** So this is like golfing and having multiple times to hit the ball to get it in the hole.
>
> **[1:24](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/python-statistics-essential-training-19258005/regression-with-xgboost?u=76281980&t=84)** That's a good analogy for XGBoost.
>
> **[1:26](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/python-statistics-essential-training-19258005/regression-with-xgboost?u=76281980&t=86)** Now, one of the nice things about XGBoost is that it uses the same interface, that scikit-learn interface.
>
> **[1:33](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/python-statistics-essential-training-19258005/regression-with-xgboost?u=76281980&t=93)** So if you have data that works in scikit-learn you can easily use XGBoost and make a model.
>
> **[1:39](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/python-statistics-essential-training-19258005/regression-with-xgboost?u=76281980&t=99)** One of the downsides of XGBoost is that it is not completely interpretable.
>
> **[1:44](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/python-statistics-essential-training-19258005/regression-with-xgboost?u=76281980&t=104)** That is to say with linear regression, we could look at the intercept and we could look at that coefficient and we could explain how the model is working.
>
> **[1:53](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/python-statistics-essential-training-19258005/regression-with-xgboost?u=76281980&t=113)** With XGBoost, it's a little bit more difficult.
>
> **[1:55](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/python-statistics-essential-training-19258005/regression-with-xgboost?u=76281980&t=115)** We have a bunch of decision trees and you could have hundreds of decision trees and going through each of those decision trees and looking at how they sum up at the end could be a little tedious.
>
> **[2:06](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/python-statistics-essential-training-19258005/regression-with-xgboost?u=76281980&t=126)** You could do it if you wanted to, but in practice no one does because it's too much work.
>
> **[2:10](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/python-statistics-essential-training-19258005/regression-with-xgboost?u=76281980&t=130)** Okay, one more thing that's useful about XGBoost is that you don't have to clean up missing values.
>
> **[2:18](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/python-statistics-essential-training-19258005/regression-with-xgboost?u=76281980&t=138)** In fact, it will work with columns that have missing values.
>
> **[2:21](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/python-statistics-essential-training-19258005/regression-with-xgboost?u=76281980&t=141)** In addition, XGBoost will also work with columns that are not numeric.
>
> **[2:26](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/python-statistics-essential-training-19258005/regression-with-xgboost?u=76281980&t=146)** So this gives it a lot of power.
>
> **[2:28](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/python-statistics-essential-training-19258005/regression-with-xgboost?u=76281980&t=148)** You can take your data and without doing a ton of pre-processing, you can throw it into an XGBoost model.
>
> **[2:34](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/python-statistics-essential-training-19258005/regression-with-xgboost?u=76281980&t=154)** It also doesn't even require your data to be standardized.
>
> **[2:38](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/python-statistics-essential-training-19258005/regression-with-xgboost?u=76281980&t=158)** So this makes XGBoost a nice model to try out and see how it performs.
>
> **[2:44](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/python-statistics-essential-training-19258005/regression-with-xgboost?u=76281980&t=164)** Here, I'm going to make x and y.
>
> **[2:47](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/python-statistics-essential-training-19258005/regression-with-xgboost?u=76281980&t=167)** Note that I am not filling in my missing values with zero, I'm just leaving those missing, and then I'm going to split my data.
>
> **[2:58](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/python-statistics-essential-training-19258005/regression-with-xgboost?u=76281980&t=178)** In this case, I will standardize it.
>
> **[3:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/python-statistics-essential-training-19258005/regression-with-xgboost?u=76281980&t=180)** It turns out that standardization does not impact XGBoost model one way or the other.
>
> **[3:06](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/python-statistics-essential-training-19258005/regression-with-xgboost?u=76281980&t=186)** It will perform the same because if you think about how a decision tree is working, it's looking at a single column, it's not comparing one column to another, and so that relative size of the columns is not really important for a tree based model.
>

#### Running statistical tests
> [LinkedIn Learning](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/python-statistics-essential-training-19258005/running-statistical-tests?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/python-statistics-essential-training-19258005/running-statistical-tests?u=76281980&t=0)** - [Instructor] In this lesson, we're going to use the scipy.stats module to run a statistical test to give us a numeric value as to whether these two distributions are the same.
>
> **[0:10](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/python-statistics-essential-training-19258005/running-statistical-tests?u=76281980&t=10)** Imported that scipy.stats module up above.
>
> **[0:14](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/python-statistics-essential-training-19258005/running-statistical-tests?u=76281980&t=14)** Scipy is a collection of different utilities for manipulating numerical data.
>
> **[0:20](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/python-statistics-essential-training-19258005/running-statistical-tests?u=76281980&t=20)** One of those modules is the stats library.
>
> **[0:23](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/python-statistics-essential-training-19258005/running-statistical-tests?u=76281980&t=23)** Let's just look and see what's in there.
>
> **[0:25](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/python-statistics-essential-training-19258005/running-statistical-tests?u=76281980&t=25)** There are a bunch of things that you can do with this stats module.
>
> **[0:31](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/python-statistics-essential-training-19258005/running-statistical-tests?u=76281980&t=31)** One of them is to run statistical tests, another one is to create distributions, there are also some plotting capabilities as well.
>
> **[0:42](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/python-statistics-essential-training-19258005/running-statistical-tests?u=76281980&t=42)** In this case, we're going to run this ks_2samp function.
>
> **[0:47](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/python-statistics-essential-training-19258005/running-statistical-tests?u=76281980&t=47)** Let's pull up the documentation for that.
>
> **[0:49](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/python-statistics-essential-training-19258005/running-statistical-tests?u=76281980&t=49)** It says, it performs a two sample Kolmogorv-Smirnov test for goodness of fit.
>
> **[0:55](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/python-statistics-essential-training-19258005/running-statistical-tests?u=76281980&t=55)** Basically what this is doing is it's testing whether these two distributions are the same.
>
> **[1:03](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/python-statistics-essential-training-19258005/running-statistical-tests?u=76281980&t=63)** Let's look at what we need to pass into it.
>
> **[1:05](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/python-statistics-essential-training-19258005/running-statistical-tests?u=76281980&t=65)** We need to pass in data1 and data2 and we already have those two series, so we should be good with that.
>
> **[1:12](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/python-statistics-essential-training-19258005/running-statistical-tests?u=76281980&t=72)** What does this return?
>
> **[1:14](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/python-statistics-essential-training-19258005/running-statistical-tests?u=76281980&t=74)** This says it returns a p-value.
>
> **[1:18](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/python-statistics-essential-training-19258005/running-statistical-tests?u=76281980&t=78)** I'm going to view as a scrollable element here and let's scroll this up.
>
> **[1:26](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/python-statistics-essential-training-19258005/running-statistical-tests?u=76281980&t=86)** This says, it returns an object containing two attributes, the KS statistic and a p-value as a result.
>
> **[1:39](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/python-statistics-essential-training-19258005/running-statistical-tests?u=76281980&t=99)** And if we scroll down a little bit, we should see some examples.
>
> **[1:42](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/python-statistics-essential-training-19258005/running-statistical-tests?u=76281980&t=102)** One of the nice things about a lot of the Python numeric libraries such as Pandas, Matplotlib, Scikit-learn and Scipy is they have really good documentation.
>
> **[1:51](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/python-statistics-essential-training-19258005/running-statistical-tests?u=76281980&t=111)** You can see an example of using it right here.
>
> **[1:54](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/python-statistics-essential-training-19258005/running-statistical-tests?u=76281980&t=114)** We just create the two distributions and then we call ks_2samp with our two samples and this gives us this KstestResult.
>
> **[2:03](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/python-statistics-essential-training-19258005/running-statistical-tests?u=76281980&t=123)** You can see that the first part here is a statistic and the next part is a p-value.
>
> **[2:09](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/python-statistics-essential-training-19258005/running-statistical-tests?u=76281980&t=129)** In this case, we're mostly interested with the p-value.
>
> **[2:13](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/python-statistics-essential-training-19258005/running-statistical-tests?u=76281980&t=133)** You can see that says, if the p-value is lower than a threshold of 0.05, we reject the null hypothesis in favor of the two-sided alternative, which is the data were not drawn from the same distribution.
>
> **[2:27](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/python-statistics-essential-training-19258005/running-statistical-tests?u=76281980&t=147)** Here's another example.
>
> **[2:28](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/python-statistics-essential-training-19258005/running-statistical-tests?u=76281980&t=148)** You can see that these two samples, the p-value is 0.5 and so because the p-value is 0.5, it's not below that threshold.
>
> **[2:39](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/python-statistics-essential-training-19258005/running-statistical-tests?u=76281980&t=159)** We cannot reject the null hypothesis or we fail to reject that.
>
> **[2:45](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/python-statistics-essential-training-19258005/running-statistical-tests?u=76281980&t=165)** The null hypothesis in this case would be that the two samples were from the same distribution, so we cannot reject that, in essence saying that we cannot prove that these were not from the same distribution, i.e. we think they are from the same distribution.
>
> **[3:03](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/python-statistics-essential-training-19258005/running-statistical-tests?u=76281980&t=183)** Let's do this with our data now.
>
> **[3:05](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/python-statistics-essential-training-19258005/running-statistical-tests?u=76281980&t=185)** I'm just going to pass in n_ames and college_cr into that and I'll print out the results there.
>
> **[3:12](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/python-statistics-essential-training-19258005/running-statistical-tests?u=76281980&t=192)** We have the statistic and the p-value.
>
> **[3:14](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/python-statistics-essential-training-19258005/running-statistical-tests?u=76281980&t=194)** The p-value is very low.
>
> **[3:16](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/python-statistics-essential-training-19258005/running-statistical-tests?u=76281980&t=196)** Down here I have an IF statement that lets us check the p-value.
>
> **[3:21](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/python-statistics-essential-training-19258005/running-statistical-tests?u=76281980&t=201)** If the p-value is greater than 0.05, we fail to reject the null hypothesis, i.e. these two are from the same distribution.
>
> **[3:29](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/python-statistics-essential-training-19258005/running-statistical-tests?u=76281980&t=209)** If it is less than 0.05, we reject the null hypothesis, meaning that they are not from the same distribution.
>
> **[3:37](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/python-statistics-essential-training-19258005/running-statistical-tests?u=76281980&t=217)** In this case, this is a very small number, it is certainly less than 0.05 and we are going to reject the null hypothesis that these two data sets are from a different distribution.
>
> **[3:50](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/python-statistics-essential-training-19258005/running-statistical-tests?u=76281980&t=230)** This confirms what we saw with both our statistical summaries and our visualizations of these two distributions.
>
> **[4:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/python-statistics-essential-training-19258005/running-statistical-tests?u=76281980&t=240)** In this lesson, we looked at using the scipy.stats library to do a statistical test to compare whether two samples were from the same distribution.
>
> **[4:09](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/python-statistics-essential-training-19258005/running-statistical-tests?u=76281980&t=249)** The scipy.stats Library makes this really easy to run statistical tests such as these and you can leverage the scipy.stats library to confirm what you see when you do visualizations or summary statistics.

>
> **[3:54](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/python-statistics-essential-training-19258005/solution-checking-square-footage-distributions?u=76281980&t=234)** Okay, remember, our null hypothesis is that the values have the same distribution.
>
> **[3:59](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/python-statistics-essential-training-19258005/solution-checking-square-footage-distributions?u=76281980&t=239)** The P-value is less than 0.05, giving us an indication that these do not have the same distribution.
>
> **[4:08](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/python-statistics-essential-training-19258005/solution-checking-square-footage-distributions?u=76281980&t=248)** In this lesson, we looked at how we can apply a Kolmogorov-Smirnov test to two samples.
